The Parable of the Young Man: Free Will and Accountability
Juwayni uses a parable to demonstrate that humans remain free and accountable even under extreme temptation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Juwayni uses a parable to affirm human free will and moral responsibility within the context of divine decree.

Core Idea
Even under extreme temptation, a person remains a free chooser accountable for their actions.

Juwayni presents a vivid hypothetical scenario to illustrate a key point in the debate on free will and predestination. The parable depicts a young man who lacks restraint yet is still considered a free agent. This lesson unpacks the parable and its implications for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the parable carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the detailed description of the young man's attributes and circumstances.
Juwayni intentionally lists many factors that would incline one to sin, yet concludes he is a free chooser.
2
Identify Juwayni's argument for free will
Find the key phrase 'he is a free chooser, not compelled' and note the role of intellect ('aql) as the basis for blame. Ask yourself: why does Juwayni insist on free will despite the lack of external restraint?
The argument hinges on the presence of choice and rationality, not on external factors.
3
Examine the Quranic support and synthesis
Read the Quranic verses Juwayni cites and his concluding synthesis. Understand how he balances divine decree with human accountability. Reflect on the role of sophistry in denying self-evident truths.
Juwayni claims that combining tafwid (delegation) with taklif (obligation) is reasonable and only sophists deny it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو فرضنا شابا حديث العهد بحلمه (۱) ، لم تهذبه المذاهب ولم تحنكه التجارب وهو على نهايته فى علمته ، وشهوته . وقد استمكن من بلغة من الحطام ، وخص بمسحة من الجمال ولم يقم عليه قوام يزعه عن ورطات الردى ، ويمنعه عن الارتباك في شبكات الهوى ، فوافاه أخدان الفساد ، وهو فى غلواء شبابه ، يحدث نفسه بالبقاء أمدا بعيدا فما أقرب من هذا وصفه من خلع العذار والبدار الى شيم الأشرار ، وهو مع ذلك كله مؤثر مختار ، ليس مجبرا على المعاصى والزلات ، ولا مصدودا عن الطاعات، ومعه من العقل ما يستوجب به اللائمة اذا عصى . فمن هذا سبيله ، لا يستحيل في العقل تكليفه ، فانه ليس ممنوعا ، ولكن ان سبق له من الله تبارك وتعالى منوع القضاء ، فهو صائر الى حكم الله الجزم ، وقضائه الفصل ، محجوجا يحجة الله تعالى الا أن يتغمده الله برحمته ، وهو أرحم الراحمين

وهذا الذى ذكرته بين في معاني الآيات لا يتمارى فيه موفق • قال تبارك وتعالى : ( ثم قست قلوبكم من بعد ذلك ) ( البقرة : ٧٤( أراد استمروا على المخالفات ، وأصروا باتنهاك الحرمات ، فقست ، وقال عز من قائل : « ولا تطع من أغفلنا قلبه عن ذكرنا ، واتبع هواه ( ) الكهف : ۲۸ ) الى غير ما ذكرناه

فقد جمعت - الله مولانا - بين تفويض الأمور كلها نفعها وضرها ، خيرها وشرها الى الاله جلت قدرته ، وبين تنقية حقائق التكليف ، وتقرير قواعد الشرائع على الوجه المعقول ، ألست في هذا --
أيضا تدل على أنه مختار ، فلا يكون وراء انكار هذا الأمر الوجداني المقطوع به . والحكم الشرعى البات غاية حميدة ، وفكرة سديدة . الا ان البشر لا يخلو من مسفسط فى أجلى البديهيات بحيث يكسوه كسوة أعوص العويصات (ز)
If we suppose a young man recently [attained] his understanding (1), not refined by schools nor seasoned by experiences, while he is at his utmost in his knowledge and his desire. And he has gained possession of a sufficiency of worldly goods, and has been endowed with a touch of beauty, and there is no restrainer to prevent him from pitfalls of ruin and to bar him from entangling in nets of passion. Then corrupt companions come upon him while he is in the full flood of his youth, telling himself that he will remain for a long time. How close is one described like this to shedding restraint and hastening to the ways of the wicked! Yet with all that, he is a free chooser, not compelled to sins and missteps, nor prevented from acts of obedience. And he possesses intellect that makes blameworthy upon him if he disobeys. One of this way, it is not impossible in reason to hold him accountable, for he is not prevented. But if there has preceded from Allah, Blessed and Exalted, a determining decree, then he will come to the decisive judgment of Allah and His conclusive decree, being refuted by the proof of Allah, Exalted, unless Allah envelops him in His mercy, and He is the Most Merciful of the merciful. 

And what I have mentioned is clear in the meanings of the verses; a successful person will not dispute it. Allah, Blessed and Exalted, said: 'Then your hearts became hardened after that' (Al-Baqara 74). He meant they continued in violations and persisted in breaking sanctities, so they hardened. And He said, Exalted is He: 'And do not obey one whose heart We have made heedless of Our remembrance and who follows his desire' (Al-Kahf 28) – to others we have not mentioned.

So I have combined – may Allah guard us – between delegating all affairs, their benefits and harms, their good and evil, to the God whose power is exalted, and between purifying the realities of obligation and establishing the rules of Sharia in a reasonable manner. Are you not in this? It also indicates that he is a free chooser. There is nothing beyond denial of this self-evident, certain matter and the final decisive legal ruling but a noble goal and a sound idea. However, humans are not free from a sophist in the clearest of self-evident matters, such that he clothes it in the garment of the most intricate difficulty (z).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage is a proof-by-parable designed to affirm human free will and accountability within the framework of divine decree. Juwayni constructs a hypothetical young man who is intellectually mature but inexperienced, endowed with knowledge, desire, wealth, and beauty, yet lacking any external restraint. When corrupt companions come, he quickly succumbs to evil. Despite all this, Juwayni insists: 'he is a free chooser (mukhtar), not compelled (majbur) to sins, nor prevented (masdud) from obedience.' He possesses intellect ('aql) that makes him blameworthy if he disobeys. Juwayni argues that such a person is rationally accountable (taklif) because he is not prevented from choosing. However, if a decree of denial (manu' al-qada) has preceded from Allah, the man will inevitably fall by Allah's decisive judgment, but he remains 'refuted by Allah's proof' – meaning his own intellect and choice serve as evidence against him, unless Allah grants mercy. Juwayni then supports his argument with Quranic verses: Surah Al-Baqara 2:74 (hearts hardened after persistence) and Surah Al-Kahf 18:28 (heedless heart). He concludes by synthesizing two principles: (1) complete delegation (tafwid) of all affairs to Allah, and (2) the reality of obligation and Sharia rules. He asserts that this synthesis is evident to reason, and the only ones who deny it are sophists who cloak the clearest self-evident truths in obscurity.

Key Terms
مختار
مجبور
تكليف
عقل
وجداني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The parable might be misread as teaching that a sinful environment determines action, so the young man is not at fault.
Correction: Juwayni emphasizes that the young man remains a free chooser and accountable because he has intellect and is not prevented from choosing obedience.

Checkpoint
In Juwayni's parable, what attributes of the young man does he emphasize to show that he is not compelled?
A) His youth and inexperience.
B) His wealth and beauty.
C) His free choice and intellect that make him blameworthy for sin.
D) His lack of a restrainer.

Key Takeaway
Juwayni's parable demonstrates that human free will and divine decree coexist, and humans are accountable based on their rational capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are alone and tempted to commit a sin that no one will see, do you truly believe that you are a free chooser accountable before Allah, or do you secretly rely on the excuse that 'it was written'? This week, when a major temptation arises, pause and say: 'I am mukhtar, not majbur—I choose, and I am accountable.' What will you do differently after that realization?
?
The young man had intellect, knowledge, and desire, yet he fell into sin when corrupt companions came. In your own life, who are your 'corrupt companions'—whether people, thoughts, or environments—that lead you to sin? How can you use your intellect, which makes you blameworthy, to distance yourself from them starting today?
?
Juwayni says that even if a decree of denial precedes from Allah, the person is 'refuted by the proof of Allah' because of their own choice. Have you ever blamed Allah's decree for a sin you committed, as if you had no choice? Repent from that thought and write down one concrete step to take full responsibility for your actions from now on.

Quranic Evidence: Hearts Harden by Choice, Not Compulsion
Al-Juwayni cites Quranic verses showing that hearts harden by choice, not compulsion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Juwayni uses two Quranic verses to argue that human hearts harden as a result of their own choices, not divine compulsion.

Core Idea
Hearts harden through persistence in sin, not by God's direct force.

Al-Juwayni now turns to Quranic evidence to support his argument that human actions are not compelled. He cites two specific verses that, when properly understood, show that hardness of heart and heedlessness are consequences of human persistence in sin. This passage demonstrates how the Quran itself, according to Juwayni, affirms human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two Quranic verses
Find the two verses cited by Juwayni: one from al-Baqarah and one from al-Kahf. Notice how he introduces them.
3
Understand Juwayni's interpretation
For each verse, note how Juwayni explains the cause of hardness or heedlessness. He links them to human actions (persistence, following desire) rather than direct divine compulsion.
4
Reflect on potential counter-argument
Consider the phrase 'We have made his heart heedless' – it might seem to imply compulsion. Juwayni's interpretation shows it is a consequence, not a direct act.
This is a key point that connects to the broader debate on qadar.

Commentary

Refuting the Denial of Human Capacity
Juwayni refutes the claim that the forsaken have no power yet are blameworthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's argument against the inconsistency of claiming that the forsaken are prevented from believing yet are still blameworthy.

Core Idea
Prevention and blame cannot coexist without contradiction.

Juwayni tackles a dangerous theological claim: that some people are divinely prevented from the truth yet are still blamed for not accepting it. He shows this undermines the very foundations of religious law. Let's examine his reasoning and the Quranic evidence he provides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic original and its English translation. Notice the rhetorical question at the beginning and the strong condemnation later.
The passage contains footnotes; ignore them for now.
2
Identify the opposing view
Juwayni describes a group that says the forsaken are 'prevented, repulsed, having no power' yet are still 'blameworthy'. Identify this claim.
This is the view he is refuting.
3
Follow Juwayni's refutation
He calls this a 'grave matter' and 'an attack on the laws'. Then he quotes verses asking 'What prevented...' to show that prevention does not remove moral responsibility.
The Quran uses 'prevention' in contexts of blame.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why Juwayni sees this as so dangerous. If God prevents and then punishes, that seems unjust. Juwayni's point is that the prevention is tied to the person's own choice, not an external coercion.
This connects to the broader debate on qadar.

Commentary
Juwayni argues against those who claim that some people are prevented (mamnūʿūn) from believing, yet still blame them. This is inconsistent and undermines the Sharia. He cites Quranic verses that use 'prevented' in a context of blame, showing that prevention is not an excuse but a consequence.

Correction: Juwayni shows that Quranic usage of 'prevention' is in blameworthy contexts, so prevention is not an excuse but a result of the person's own choice.

Key Takeaway
Juwayni refutes the claim that the forsaken are divinely prevented yet still blameworthy, arguing that such prevention does not negate moral responsibility.

Refuting the Claim of Divine Compulsion
Al-Juwayni refutes the claim of divine compulsion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Juwayni's refutation of the claim that God compels sin and why it constitutes confusion about divine attributes.

Core Idea
Forcing sin contradicts God's wisdom and lordship.

In this passage, Imam al-Juwayni addresses a dangerous claim: that the servant sins while God is the compelling agent. He labels this as confusion in the judgments of divinity and interference with lordship. Through a logical argument, he shows that if God had not willed their sin, He would not have created them with the capacity to sin. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the overall argument Juwayni is making.
Notice the strong language: 'confusion' and 'interference'.
2
Identify the claim and response
Identify the claim being refuted (the servant sins, God compels) and Juwayni's immediate response: it is confusion in divinity.
3
Analyze the logical argument
Follow the conditional argument: if God did not will their sin, He would not have created them; but He did create them and gave them means, so He willed it.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Consider the objection
Notice the phrase 'If it is said: He did that to them' — this sets up an objection that will be answered in the following discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت طوائف من الضلال : الى أن العبد يعصى ، والرب لما : باتی به کاره ، فهذا خبط فى أحكام الالهية ، ومزاحمة في الربوبية ولو لم يرد الرب من الفجار ما علمه منهم فى أزله ، لما فطرهم مع كيف ؟ وقد أكمل قواهم ، وأمدهم بالعدد ، والعدد ، بهم علة بها والعتاد ، وسهل طريق العيد عن السداد . فإن قيل : فعل ذلك بهم
And groups of the misguided went to say that the servant sins and the Lord does it against his will? This is confusion in the judgments of divinity and interference with lordship. If the Lord had not willed from the wicked what He knew from them in eternity, He would not have created them, and how? He has already perfected their powers, supplied them with numbers and tools, and facilitated the path of deviation from rectitude. If it is said: He did that to them.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
Juwayni refutes the view that God compels sin while the servant is blamed. He argues that if God did not will their sin, He would not have created them with the capacity to sin. The fact that He gave them powers and means shows that sin is a result of their choice. This passage is an objection that is then answered in the following discussion.

Key Terms
طوائف من الضلال
خبط في أحكام الالهية
مزاحمة في الربوبية
إكمال القوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills sin, then He forces the servant to sin, making the servant blameless.
Correction: Juwayni refutes this by showing that God's will and provision do not remove human choice; the servant sins by his own acquisition.

Checkpoint

A) It is correct
B) It is confusion in divinity
C) It is a metaphor
D) It is an exaggeration

Key Takeaway
The claim that God compels sin is a confusion of divine attributes and lordship, as God's will and provision do not negate human responsibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Think of a sin you struggle with. How does God's giving you powers and tools (as the passage says) make your repentance more urgent—and your excuse less valid?

You Do Not Will Unless God Wills: Human Will and Divine Creation
Al-Juwayni explains that the verse does not imply compulsion but affirms human choice within divine creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Juwayni's argument that the Qur'anic verse 'You do not will unless God wills' affirms human choice while denying that humans generate their own will independently, thus refuting compulsion.

Core Idea
Your will is real, but God creates it in you.

Al-Juwayni tackles a key Qur'anic verse that might seem to suggest compulsion (jabr). He shows that it actually affirms human will as something God creates within us, not something we generate independently. This is the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb (acquisition). In this passage, he uses the method of interpreting the Qur'an by the Qur'an and warns against the delusion of self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from one verse to another.
The passage uses two 'texts' from the Qur'an to establish human will.
2
Identify the Two Movements
First, Juwayni cites verses that affirm human will ('whoever wills takes a path', 'whoever wills to be upright'). Then he cites 'You do not will unless God wills' to show that the will itself is created by God. Observe how he warns against delusion before the correction.
The warning is a pedagogical tool.
3
Reflect on the Analogy
Juwayni compares the verse to 'Do not confer favor upon me for your Islam; rather, God confers favor upon you that He guided you.' This shows that the verse is not about compulsion but about recognizing that all good comes from God.
The analogy clarifies the meaning.
4
Connect to Kasb
Consider how this passage supports the Ash'ari doctrine of kasb: humans have genuine will and action, but God creates the will and the action. The verse does not negate human agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله تعالى : ( فمن شاء اتخذ الى ربه سبيلا » نص فى أن اتخاذ السبيل إلى مشيئة ان يستقيم » محمول على ذلك النص فى أن الاستقامة الى مشيئة العبد أيضا تفسيرا للقرآن بالقرآن وهذه مظنة اغترار العبد واعتزازه يعلمه وهذا ما يغار عليه الله سبحانه مولى الفضل كله فأدب عبده زاجرا له عن ذلك بقوله ، « وما تشاءون الا أن يشاء الله » يعنى أنه لولا ان الله خلقكم شنائين مختارين ما كان لكم قصد يترتب عليه اتخاذ السبيل والاستقامة ولا كنتم تشاءون هذا ولا ذاك فاذن الفضل كله الله جل شأنه . فتكون الآية من قبيل « لا تمنوا على اسلامكم ، بل الله يمن عليكم أن هذاكم » فلا تكون لآية المشيئة أيضا بالجبر أصلا ظاهر لمن كان له قلب ، أو القى السمع وهو شهيد (ز)
And His saying: 'So whoever wills let him take a path to his Lord' is text indicating that taking the path is to the will of the servant. And 'Whoever among you wills to be upright' is carried upon that, text indicating that uprightness is also to the will of the servant. This is interpretation of the Quran by the Quran. And this is a place where the servant may be deluded and proud of his knowledge. And this is something that God, the possessor of all bounty, is jealous over. So He disciplined His servant by restraining him from that by His saying: 'And you do not will unless God wills,' meaning that if God had not created you as willing and choosing, you would have no intention that results in taking the path and uprightness, nor would you will this or that. So all excellence is from God Almighty. So this verse is of the type 'Do not confer favor upon me for your Islam; rather, God confers favor upon you that He guided you.' So the verse of willing is also not for compulsion at all, clear for one who has a heart or gives ear while being a witness.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
Juwayni interprets key Quranic verses to show that human will is real but dependent on God's creation of that will. He rejects the interpretation that 'you do not will unless God wills' negates human agency. Instead, it affirms that God creates the will in humans as choosers. This is the Ash'ari doctrine of acquisition (kasb).

Key Terms
مشيئة
الاستقامة
الجبر
المنة
الكسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'You do not will unless God wills' means humans have no real will and are forced by God to act.
Correction: Al-Juwayni explains that God creates the will in humans as choosers, so human will is real but dependent on God's creation. The verse does not negate human agency but rather affirms that the ability to will is a gift from God.

Checkpoint
According to Juwayni, what does the verse 'And you do not will unless God wills' mean?
A) Humans have no real will
B) God creates the will in humans as choosers
C) God forces humans to will
D) Humans are independent

Key Takeaway
The verse teaches that human will is a created gift from God, not a denial of human agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that your own will to do good is a gift from God, not a product of your own power. When you pray, do you sincerely ask God to create the will in you to obey Him, or do you assume you can will it on your own? Starting tonight, add to your dua a request for Allah to create in you the will to seek His pleasure.
?
When you accomplish a good deed, do you feel a subtle pride in your own effort? Al-Juwayni warns that the deluded servant might become proud. Search your heart: have you ever taken credit for your own willingness? How will you remind yourself daily that 'all excellence is from God'? Write down one specific moment today when you will consciously attribute your intention to Allah.
?
This lesson teaches that the verse 'You do not will unless God wills' is a discipline from God against pride. What if you never recalculated your own will in light of this? Would your religious life be one of arrogance rather than gratitude? Commit to a weekly practice of reflecting on where your will to be upright comes from.

The Problem of the Forsaken Being Prevented Yet Blameworthy
Juwayni states the problem of the forsaken being prevented yet blameworthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological problem raised by Juwayni regarding the forsaken being both prevented from responding and still blameworthy, and why he considers it a grave attack on divine laws.

Core Idea
Prevention and blame can coexist in divine action, challenging shallow compatibilism.

Juwayni confronts a disturbing claim: that those whom God forsakes are prevented from responding to the call of truth, yet they are still held blameworthy. This seems contradictory to common sense, but the author argues that denying this tension leads to an attack on the very foundations of divine laws and prophetic calls. In this lesson, we will unpack his reasoning and the Quranic evidence he cites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the flow: from a rhetorical question to the identification of a group's claim, then Juwayni's strong condemnation, and finally Quranic citations.
Pay attention to the phrase 'خطب جسيم' as it sets the tone.
2
Identify the problematic claim
The passage states that some people say the forsaken (المخذولون) are prevented and repelled, having no power to respond to the callers of truth, yet they are still blamed. This is the core issue.
This claim appears to make God unjust if He punishes those He prevents.
3
Understand Juwayni's condemnation
Juwayni declares this view a 'grave calamity and great matter' and says it is an attack on the divine laws and an invalidation of the calls. He sees it as undermining the entire structure of religious obligation.
Reflect on why this would be considered so destructive.
4
Examine the Quranic citations
Juwayni quotes two verses: 'What prevented people from believing' (18:55) and 'What prevented you from prostrating' (38:75). These show that the concept of prevention (man') appears in the Quran, linking to the issue.
These verses are used to argue that prevention does not automatically remove moral responsibility.
5
Reflect on the gravity of the issue
The lesson ends with a prayer seeking refuge from bad reasoning in perilous places. Consider why this issue is perilous and how it relates to the broader themes of divine justice and human responsibility.
This sets the stage for Juwayni's subsequent arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أهدى سبيلا، وأقوم قيلا، ممن يقدر الطبع منها، والختم صدا ودفعا؟ ثم ينفى التكاليف بزعمه، وقد افترق الخلق في هذا المقام فرقا، فذهب ذاهبون الى أن المخذولين ممنوعون مدفوعون لا اقتدار لهم على اجابة دعاة الحق. وهم مع ذلك ملومون. وهذا خطب جسيم، وأمر عظيم، وهو طعن في الشرائع، وابطال للدعوات. وقال تبارك وتعالى: ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا ) وقال لابليس: ( ما منعك أن تسجد ) نعوذ بالله من سوء النظر فى مواضع الخطر
More guided and truer in speech than one who determines nature from it, and the seal as rust and repulsion? Then he denies obligations by his claim. And people have differed in this matter into groups. Some went to the view that the forsaken are prevented, repelled, having no power to respond to the callers of truth. Yet they are blameworthy. This is a grave calamity and a great matter, and it is an attack on the divine laws and an invalidation of the calls. And the Blessed and Most High said: 'And what prevented people from believing' (al-Kahf 55). And He said to Iblis: 'What prevented you from prostrating' (Sad 75). We seek refuge with Allah from bad reasoning in perilous places.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage identifies the first problematic group's claim: that those whom God forsakes are prevented from responding to the call of truth and have no ability to respond, yet they are still blameworthy. The author declares this a grave matter, an attack on the divine laws (sharā'iʿ) and a nullification of the prophetic calls. He then cites Qur'anic verses (al-Kahf 55) and the story of Iblis (Ṣād 75) to show that the question of prevention is raised in the Qur'an. The passage ends with a prayer for refuge from error in such perilous matters.

Key Terms
المخذولون
التكاليف
دعاة الحق
الشرائع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God prevents someone from doing something, then it is unjust to blame them for not doing it.
Correction: Juwayni shows that the forsaken are both prevented and blameworthy, and that this is not a contradiction; rather, denying it would undermine the divine laws. The Quranic verses indicate that prevention and blame can coexist.

Checkpoint
According to Juwayni, what is the problem with the claim that the forsaken are prevented from responding yet are blameworthy?
A) It is a minor issue.
B) It is a grave attack on the divine laws.
C) It is a position held by the righteous.
D) It is easily resolved.

Key Takeaway
Juwayni establishes that the coexistence of divine prevention and human blame is a serious theological problem that, if misunderstood, undermines the entire structure of religious obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used God's decree as an excuse for your own failure to act righteously? How does Juwayni's insistence that the forsaken are both prevented and blameworthy strip away that excuse from your heart? Commit today to take full responsibility for your actions, even when you feel divinely hindered.
?

Let this passage confront you: will you trust God's justice even when you cannot comprehend how prevention and blame coexist? Pray for humility to accept the mystery.
?
Is there a command of Allah that you find difficult because you feel 'prevented' by your circumstances or nature? How will you respond to Juwayni's warning that denying obligation due to prevention is an attack on the divine laws? Make a concrete plan this week to obey despite feeling hindered.

God's Will Encompasses All Things, Including Sin
Juwayni refutes the claim that God does not will sin and defends the orthodox position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's refutation of the claim that God does not will sin, and the orthodox position that God wills all that occurs, including sin, in accordance with His pre-eternal knowledge.

Core Idea
God's will is all-encompassing; He wills even sin, yet remains just and wise.

This passage addresses a dangerous misconception: that sin occurs against God's will. Juwayni exposes the logical contradictions in this view and defends the orthodox Ash'ari position that God's will is comprehensive. He uses a powerful analogy and concludes with the balanced stance of the 'successful ones' (al-muwaffaqūn).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Juwayni's strong language: 'confusion' and 'rivalry'.
2
Identify the deviant claim and its implications
The deviants claim that the servant sins while God does not will the sin. Juwayni says this leads to confusion about divinity. Ask yourself: why would this be a problem for God's lordship?
The deviant view implies that God creates sinners without willing their sin, which Juwayni finds absurd.
3
Understand the analogy of the master and servant
Juwayni presents an analogy: a master who knows his servant will become a tyrant if given wealth, yet gives him the wealth claiming he wants good deeds. This would make the master corrupting his servant. Understand how this parallels the deviant view.
The analogy shows that if God knows someone will sin but still creates and enables them with the claim He wants obedience, He would be acting unjustly.
4
Recognize the two misguided groups and the orthodox position
Juwayni then points out two groups that went astray: one that rejects divine rulings (probably the Mu'tazila who deny God's will for sin) and another that rivals God's lordship (likely the Jabriyya who deny human power). The successful ones (al-muwaffaqūn) say God wills what He knows, without stripping humans of their ability.
The orthodox position balances divine will and human responsibility.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Consider how Juwayni's conclusion upholds both Shari'a (human accountability) and divine lordship. Why is this balance important?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذهبت طوائف من الضلال: الى أن العبد يعصى، والرب [؟] باتي به کاره، فهذا خبط فى أحكام الالهية، ومزاحمة في الربوبية. ولو لم يرد الرب من الفجار ما علمه منهم فى أزله، لما فطرهم مع كيف؟ وقد أكمل قواهم، وأمدهم بالعدد، والعدد، والعتاد، وسهل طريق العيد عن السداد. فإن قيل: فعل ذلك ليطيعوه. قلنا: أنى يستقيم ذلك؟ وقد علم أنهم يعصونه ويهلكون أنفسهم، ويهلكون أولياء، وأنبياء، ويشقون شقاوة لا يسعدون بعدها أبدا. ولو علم سيد عن وحى، أو اخبار نبى أنه لو أمد عبده بالمال لطغى، وأبق وقطع الطريق فأمده بالمال، زاعما أنه يريد منة ابتناء القناطر والمقابر والمساجد، وهو مع ذلك: يقول: اعلم أنه لا يفعل ذلك قطعا، فهذا السيد مفسد عبده وليس مصلحا له، با نفاق من أرباب الألباب، فقد زاغت الفئتان، وضلت الفرقتان، اعترضت إحداهما على القواعد الشرعية: وزاحمت الأخرى أحكام الزبونية. واقتصد الموفقون فقالوا: أراد الله تعالى من عباده ما علم أنهم اليه يصيرون، والله لم يسلبهم قدرهم، ولم يمنعهم مراشدهم. فقرت الشريعة فى نصابها، وجرت العقيدة في الأحكام الالهية على صوابها.
And groups of deviation went to the view that the servant sins, and the Lord [؟] does it unwillingly? This is confusion in divine rulings and rivalry in lordship. And if the Lord had not willed from the wicked what He knew from them in His pre-eternity, He would not have created them—how? He completed their powers, supplied them with numbers and equipment, and made easy the path of the servant/slave toward rectitude. If it is said: He did that so that they would obey Him. We say: How can that be correct? He knows that they will disobey Him and destroy themselves, and destroy friends and prophets, and suffer misery after which they will never be happy. If a master knew through revelation or prophetic report that if he provided his servant with wealth he would become tyrannical, rebellious, and a highway robber, but he provided him with wealth claiming that he wants the favor of building bridges, tombs, and mosques, while saying: Know that he will definitely not do that—then this master is corrupting his servant, not reforming him. O discerning ones, the two groups have deviated and the two sects have gone astray: one objected to the divine rules, the other competed with the rulings of lordship. The successful ones took a middle course and said: Allah Almighty willed from His servants what He knew they would become, and Allah did not strip them of their power nor prevent their means of guidance. So the Shari'a stood firm in its place, and the creed regarding divine rulings proceeded correctly.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage deals with another group of deviants who claim that the servant sins while the Lord does not will it unwillingly (the text is unclear). The author says this is confusion in divine rulings and rivalry with lordship. He then presents their argument: if the Lord had not willed from the wicked what He knew in pre-eternity, He would not have created them with complete faculties, provided them with numbers and equipment, and made the path of servitude easy. Then an objection is raised: He did that so that they would obey. The author responds: How can that be when He knows they will disobey and cause destruction? He gives an analogy of a master who gives wealth to a servant knowing he will become a tyrant, yet claims he wants him to build bridges and mosques—this master is corrupting his servant, not reforming him. This leads to two misguided groups: one objecting to the divine laws, the other competing with divine lordship. The correct view (al-muwaffaqūn) is that God willed from His servants what He knew they would become, and He did not deprive them of their ability nor prevent their guidance. Thus the Shari'a stands firm and creed is correct.

Key Terms
الضلال
أزله
الموفقون
القدر
الشريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God does not will sin; sin occurs against His will.
Correction: Juwayni demonstrates that God's will encompasses all things, including sin, because He created sinners with full knowledge of their actions. If He did not will their sin, He would not have created them with the capacities that lead to sin.

Checkpoint
What is the analogy Juwayni uses to refute the claim that God created people for obedience despite knowing their disobedience?
A) A master who gives wealth to a servant knowing he will rebel.
B) A king who builds a palace.
C) A father who disciplines his son.
D) A judge who pardons a criminal.

Key Takeaway
The orthodox position affirms that God wills all that He knows, including sin, but He does not deprive humans of their ability or guidance, thus preserving both divine sovereignty and human accountability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you silently blame God's will—'It was written'—as an excuse? This passage teaches that God wills your sin yet you still have power. Will you tonight own your sin fully before God, without evading responsibility?
?
Juwayni's analogy of the master shows that enabling someone despite knowing they will do evil is corruption. Is there a person in your life you enable in sin by not speaking truth? Will you confront them this week, trusting God's wisdom over your comfort?
?
The balanced position affirms both God's sovereignty and your accountability. Which side do you lean toward: excusing your sin by God's decree, or denying His will in fear of injustice? How will you repent of that imbalance today?

Divine Decree

The Master-Slave Analogy and Divine Decree
Juwayni refutes an implicit objection using a human analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Juwayni's refutation of the objection that God's command to obey is undermined by His foreknowledge of disobedience.

Core Idea
A master who enables rebellion knowing it will happen corrupts, not reforms.

Juwayni confronts a subtle objection: if God knows that people will disobey, why does He still command them to obey? A human master who acts similarly would be corrupting his slave. This lesson unpacks Juwayni's powerful analogy and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening objection
Begin with the implicit objection: How can God command obedience when He knows of disobedience? Juwayni responds with 'How can that be sound?'
Notice the rhetorical question sets up the refutation.
2
Examine the master-slave analogy
Juwayni gives an example of a master who gives wealth to a slave knowing he will rebel. Ask yourself: why is this master corrupting the slave?
The key is that the master claims good intentions but knows the outcome.
3
Connect the analogy to the objection
The analogy implies that if God commanded obedience while knowing of disobedience, He would be like that master. Juwayni's point is that this reasoning is flawed because God is not a human master.
The analogy is a reductio ad absurdum, not a literal comparison.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليطيعوه . قلنا : أنى يستقيم ذلك ؟ وقد علم أنهم يعصونه ويهلكون أنفسهم ، ويهلكون أولياء ، وأنبياء ، ويشقون شقاوة لا يسعدون بعدها أبدا ولو علم سيد عن وحى ، أو اخبار نبى أنه لو أمد عبده بالمال لطغى ، وأبق وقطع الطريق فأمده بالمال ، زاعما أنه يريد منة ابتناء القناطر والمقابر والمساجد ، وهو مع ذلك : يقول : اعلم أنه لا يفعل ذلك قطعا ، فهذا السيد مفسد عبده وليس مصلحا له ، با نفاق من أرباب الألباب ، فقد زاغت الفئتان ، وضلت الفرقتان ، اعترضت إحداهما على القواعد الشرعية : وزاحمت الأخرى أحكام الزبونية واقتصد الموفقون فقالوا : أراد الله تعالى من عباده ما علم أنهم اليه يصيرون ، والله لم يسلبهم قدرهم ، ولم يمنعهم مراشدهم . فقرت الشريعة فى نصابها ، وجرت العقيدة في الأحكام الالهية على صوابها .
ذلك
فان قيل : كيف يريد الحكيم السفه ؟ فقد سبق في قدر كاف شاف ، لصدر كل ذى لب . وأوضحنا : أن الأفعال متساوية في حق من لا ينتفع ولا يتضرر . ولكن اذا أخبر أنه مكلف مطالب عنادة ، مزيح عائلهم ، فقوله الحق ، وكلامه الصدق . وأقرب أمر بعا رضوان به : أن الحكيم منا اذا رأى جواريه وعبيده يموج بعضهم في بعض ، وهم على محارمهم بمرآة منه ومسمع ، فلا يحسن تركهم على ما هم عليه (1) . والرب مطلع على سوء أفعال العباد ويستدرجهم من حيث لا يعلمون .
وقد أطلت أنفاسي قليلا . ولكن - حرس الله مولانا - لو وجدت فى اقتباس هذا العلم من يسرد لى هذا الفصل لكان - وحق القائم على كل نفس بما كسبت - أحب الى من ملك الدنيا بحذافيرها طول أمدها .
(1) لكن الله سبحانه لا يقاس بأحد ، فلا يكون ضرب المثل بحكيم من البشر غير خطأ على خطأ (ز)
So that they may obey Him. We said: How can that be sound? He knows that they will disobey Him and destroy themselves, and destroy saints and prophets, and experience misery never to be happy after it. Even if a master knew through revelation or prophetic report that if he gave wealth to his slave, he would become rebellious, run away, and cut the road, but he gives him wealth, claiming he wants the favor of building bridges, tombs, and mosques, while also saying: Know that he will certainly not do that — this master is corrupting his slave, not reforming him. This is hypocrisy from those of understanding. So both factions deviated, both groups went astray: one objected to the legal rules, the other clashed with the rulings of divine lordship. The successful ones took the middle path, saying: Allah willed from His servants what He knew they would end up to, and Allah did not deprive them of their capability nor prevent them from their guidance. So the Law remained in its proper place, and the creed regarding divine judgments proceeded correctly.
That
If it is said: How can the Wise will foolishness? This has already been covered in sufficient decree for every intelligent person. And we have clarified: Actions are equal with respect to One who neither benefits nor suffers. But when He informs that He has tasked and demanded, removing their excuses, His statement is truth and His words are truth. The closest matter to acceptance is that a wise person among us, when he sees his maids and slaves surging among each other while they are upon forbidden acts within his sight and hearing, it is not fitting to leave them as they are. And the Lord is aware of the evil deeds of the servants and gradually draws them on from where they do not know.
I have prolonged my breath a little. But - may God protect our master - if I found in the acquisition of this knowledge someone who would recite this chapter to me - by the right of the One who stands over every soul for what it has earned - it would be more beloved to me than possessing the entire world for its entire duration.
(1) But Allah is not measured against anyone, so drawing an analogy with a wise person among humans is nothing but error upon error.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and their refutations regarding divine decree and human actions. First, the author responds to an implicit objection: if God commands obedience but knows people will disobey, how is that sound? He gives the example of a master who knows his slave will rebel if given wealth, yet gives it while claiming he wants good deeds. Such a master is corrupting his slave, not reforming him. The author then notes that two groups went astray: one opposed the Sharia rulings, the other clashed with divine lordship. The successful group took the middle path: Allah willed what He knew they would become, without stripping them of capability or guidance. Then a second explicit objection is raised: 'How can the Wise will foolishness?' The author responds that actions are equal for God who neither benefits nor suffers, but when He informs that He has tasked and demanded, His word is truth. He uses an analogy: a wise human master seeing his slaves committing sins in full view would not leave them unchecked. However, a footnote immediately corrects this analogy, stating that God is not comparable to any human, so such an analogy is error upon error. The author then laments the loneliness of this science, wishing for someone to study it with him.

Key Terms
قدر (divine decree)
تكليف (moral obligation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God commands obedience despite knowing disobedience, He is like a corrupt human master.
Correction: The analogy only serves as a reductio ad absurdum; God is not comparable to humans, and His actions are just and wise beyond full human comprehension.

Checkpoint
What does Juwayni's master-slave analogy illustrate?
A) God is like a human master who can be corrupt.
B) The objection leads to the conclusion that God would be corrupting His servants, which is false.
C) Slaves should always obey their masters.
D) Building mosques is always a good deed.

Key Takeaway
Juwayni refutes the objection by showing its absurd consequence through a human analogy, then affirms that Allah's decree and human responsibility coexist without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever catch yourself thinking 'It was written' as an excuse, instead of owning your choice? After reading this passage, how will you respond the next time that thought arises?
?
If you knew that giving someone money would lead them into greater sin, would you still give it? How does that change the way you view God's permission of evil—and how do you need to trust His wisdom even when you don't understand?
?
You claim to believe in both divine decree and human responsibility, but which one do you really rely on when life is hard—your own effort or a fatalistic surrender? What concrete step will you take today to live out the middle path this lesson teaches?

The Middle Path: Divine Will and Human Responsibility
Juwayni presents the middle path and responds to the 'Wise and foolishness' objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct position between those who reject Sharia and those who reject divine lordship, and how Juwayni responds to the objection 'How can the Wise will foolishness?'

Core Idea
Actions are equal for God; He is not subject to benefit or harm.

Juwayni continues by identifying two extreme groups and presenting the middle path. Then he faces a second objection: How can the Wise (al-Hakim) will foolishness? His response hinges on the foundational principle that God's actions are not comparable to human actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two deviant groups
One group clashed with Sharia, the other with divine lordship. Notice how they are both wrong.
The middle path avoids both extremes.
2
Consider the second objection
The objection asks: How can the Wise will foolishness? It implies that commanding obedience while knowing of disobedience is foolish.
This objection challenges God's wisdom.
3
Grasp Juwayni's foundational response
Juwayni states that actions are equal in relation to God because He neither benefits nor suffers. This means human standards of wisdom and foolishness do not apply to God in the same way.
This is a key theological principle: God is beyond human measurements.

The Limits of Analogies: Divine Transcendence
Juwayni provides a footnote correcting the human master analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why analogies between God and human beings must be strictly limited and corrected to preserve divine transcendence.

Core Idea
God is not comparable to any human; analogies are error upon error.

Juwayni uses a human analogy to explain divine wisdom, but immediately qualifies it. A footnote makes it explicit: Allah is not to be compared to anyone. This lesson focuses on that crucial correction and its implications for theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read the analogy and its correction
First read the analogy of the human master (from previous lesson). Then read the footnote: 'But Allah is not measured against anyone, so drawing an analogy with a wise person among humans is nothing but error upon error.'
Notice that Juwayni himself warns against taking the analogy too far.
2
Understand why the analogy is limited
The analogy helps illustrate a point, but it cannot capture God's transcendence. Human masters are fallible and limited; God is not.
This principle is essential in Islamic theology: tanzih (transcendence).
3
Reflect on the theological method
Juwayni uses analogies from human experience but always corrects them. This shows a balanced approach: use reason but never compromise divine uniqueness.
This method is typical of Ash'ari kalam.

What does the footnote say about the analogy of a human master?
A) It is a perfect analogy for God.
B) It is beneficial if understood correctly.

D) It applies only to human masters, not to God.

Key Takeaway
Analogies with human actions are useful but must always be corrected to avoid compromising divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him reacting like a human? What would it cost you to truly internalize that He is beyond all comparison?
?
If the analogy of a master and slave is 'error upon error' when applied to God, how carefully do you guard your own thoughts about Allah's nature? Where might you be slipping into hidden anthropomorphism without realizing it?
?
How does the reality of divine transcendence change the way you approach the problem of evil? Will you let this lesson reshape your trust in God's wisdom, even when His ways are beyond your understanding?

Deviant Views on Reward and Punishment
Juwayni reports deviant views on reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sunni theologians reject the view that reward is obligatory upon God, recognizing this as a deviation from proper divine attributes.

Core Idea
Reward is not a debt owed to us; it is a gift from Allah's grace.

In the fourth pillar of his creed, Imam Juwayni identifies a core error made by certain deviant sects: they claimed that if a person obeys God, it becomes compulsory for God to reward them. This lesson unpacks that claim and why it is considered misguided. We read Juwayni's precise critique and see how it safeguards divine freedom and wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's own phrasing settle before interpreting.
Notice how Juwayni labels the groups as 'people of deviation and misguidance'.
2
Identify the deviant claim
The passage states two parts: (1) about obedience (reward is obligatory) and (2) about disobedience (confusion). Identify each part and note the key phrase 'وجوب الحكمة' (necessity of wisdom).
The deviant groups use 'wisdom' to argue that God must reward.
3
Reflect on why this is problematic
Ask yourself: If reward is obligatory on God, what does that imply about God's freedom and grace? The Sunni stance is that God is not compelled by any external necessity. This will connect to the learning objective.
Contrast with the Sunni belief that all reward is from God's bounty, not obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الرابع : يتعلق بالثواب والعقاب . ذهبت طوائف من أهل الزيغ والضلال ، الى أن العبد اذا أطاع ربه ، وجب على الله تبارك وتعالى أن يثيبه وجوب الحكمة . فإن عصاه اضطربوا فى حكم الاله .

Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth pillar, which deals with reward (thawab) and punishment (iqaab). The author reports that certain deviant groups hold that if the servant obeys Allah, it becomes obligatory (wajib) upon Allah to reward him, due to the necessity of wisdom (wujub al-hikma). However, if the servant disobeys, these groups are confused and disagree about what God's judgment should be (whether punishment is obligatory or not). The author labels these groups as 'people of deviation and misguidance' (ahl al-zaygh wa al-dalal), indicating that this view is not correct. The passage does not provide the author's own alternative view but simply states the position of these groups as unsound.

Correction: Juwayni labels this view as deviant; in Sunni theology, reward is a gift from God's grace, not an obligation upon Him.

Condition of Intellect for Divine Address
Juwayni defines the necessity of intellect for valid divine address.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellect is a necessary condition for a valid divine command and how it differs from the condition of puberty.

Core Idea
Without intellect, divine command becomes impossible burden.

Imam Juwayni continues his discussion on the pillars of tawhid by addressing the second pillar: the condition of intellect for taklif. He argues that a valid command requires the servant to possess intellect to understand the command; otherwise, it would be an impossible obligation. He further distinguishes between the intellect condition for initial address and the puberty condition for continuity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to Juwayni's logical flow.
The passage is a self-contained argument.
2
Identify the condition of intellect
Find where Juwayni states that intellect must be present for the servant to understand the command. Why is understanding necessary?
3
Understand the impossible obligation
Juwayni argues that obligating someone who cannot understand is an impossible burden (taklif ma la yutaq). What analogy does he use?
He compares it to obligating beasts and inanimate objects.
4
Distinguish intellect from puberty
Notice how Juwayni separates the condition of intellect (for initial address) from the condition of puberty (for continuity of obligation). What is the basis of each?
Intellect is rational; puberty is based on divine law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الثاني : من القول في هذا ، وهو مقتضب مما تقدم ، قريب المأخذ بعد الاحاطة بما سبق . وذلك أن يشترط في توجيه التكليف على العبد حضور عقله الذي يستمكن به من فهم الخطاب . اذ لو لم يكن كذلك - لا يتصور قصد امتثال الأمر قبل فهمه والعلم بالآمر تعالى - والا كان ذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . وهو مستحيل . وتقريب القول فيه : أن من ضرورة توجه الأمر على المخاطب : تكليفه الخطاب . وتكليف من يستحيل أن يفهم الأمر محال . وهو بمثابة تكليف البهائم والجمادات ، ولا معنى لبسط الكلام في الجليات . وأما البلوغ فهو مشروط العقل في استمرار التكليف ، ولكن مدارك شرطه : مع الشرع . ولو رددناه الى العقل لم يستحل في مقتضاه تكليف العاقل من الصبيان
The second pillar: of discourse on this, and it is a summary of what precedes, close in derivation after encompassing what came before. That is: it is required in directing obligation (taklif) upon the servant that his intellect be present, by which he can understand the address. For if it were not so—it is not conceivable to intend compliance with the command before understanding it and knowledge of the Commander, Exalted—otherwise that would be an impossible obligation (taklif ma la yutaq). And that is impossible. And a clarification of the statement therein: indeed, from the necessity of directing the command to the addressee is: obligating him with the address. And obligating one for whom understanding the command is impossible is absurd. It is like obligating beasts and inanimate objects, and there is no meaning to elaborating speech on clear matters. As for reaching puberty (bulugh), it is conditioned by intellect for the continuity of obligation, but the basis of its condition is with the Law (shar'). If we were to refer it to reason, it would not be impossible in its implication to obligate a rational child (al-‘aqil min al-sibyan).
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the second pillar of tawhid concerning divine address. Imam Juwayni states that for a command to be validly directed to a servant, the servant must possess an intellect capable of comprehending the command. Without intellect, compliance is impossible because the servant cannot understand the command or know the Commander. Such an obligation would be an impossible burden (taklif ma la yutaq), which is impossible for God. Juwayni emphasizes that obligating someone who cannot understand is like obligating animals or inanimate objects—plainly absurd. He then distinguishes between the condition of intellect for the initial address and the condition of reaching puberty (bulugh) for continuity of obligation; the latter is based on revealed law (shar‘), not reason alone. If reason were the sole criterion, it would not be impossible to obligate a rational child (who has not reached puberty).

Key Terms
تكليف
عقل
خطاب
تكليف ما لا يطاق
بلوغ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellect alone, without divine law, determines the onset of religious obligation.
Correction: Juwayni clarifies that while intellect is necessary for understanding the command, the condition of puberty (bulugh) is based on divine law (shar'), not reason. Reason alone would allow obligating rational children, which is not the case.

Checkpoint

A) Reaching puberty
B) Presence of intellect
C) Being a Muslim
D) Being an adult

Key Takeaway
For a divine command to be valid, the servant must have intellect to understand it; otherwise, it becomes an impossible obligation, which is contrary to Allah's justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If you were to be judged on how you used your ability to comprehend divine address, would you be found negligent? What will you change starting now?

Third Pillar: Possibility of the Commanded Act
Juwayni asserts that the commanded act must be inherently possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic theology holds that God's commands must be possible for the servant to perform, and how this principle excludes commands like combining opposites or disbelief.

Core Idea
God only commands what is possible — the impossible is not a valid test.

Juwayni concludes the discussion of the three pillars of obligation by establishing the third: the commanded act must be inherently possible. He provides concrete examples of what cannot be commanded — combining opposites, being in two places, or disbelief — and explains the logical contradiction in commanding disbelief. This passage also unifies the three pillars under a single creedal principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third pillar
Read the definition: the commanded act must be possible in itself (mumkin) both in existence and occurrence. This is a necessary condition for valid taklif.
Notice the term 'mumkin' — it refers to what is rationally possible, not just physically.
2
Examine the examples of impossible commands
Juwayni gives three examples: combining two opposites, being in two places at once, and commanding disbelief. For each, identify why it is impossible.
The disbelief example is explained in more detail — it involves a contradiction in the commanded person's knowledge.
3
Grasp the unifying principle
The three pillars rest on the single principle (qa‘idat al-‘aqidah) that the servant is only required with what is possible (ja’iz), not impossible. This limits both God’s decree and His commands.
This principle safeguards divine justice and human responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الثالث : أن يكون المأمور به ممكنا في نفسه وجودا ووقوعا فلا يجوز ورود التكليف بجمع الضدين والكون في مكانين في وقت واحد ، ويستحيل ورود الأمر والكفر بالله تبارك وتعالى لأن من ضرورة تصوير الأمر فهم المأمور الأمر وعلمه بالأمر ، وكيف يتصور مع العلم بالله ذي الأمر ، الجهل به ؟ فهو من قبيل جمع الضدين فقد خرجت هذه الأركان الثلاثة على أصل واحد هو قاعدة العقيدة ، أن العبد ، مطالب بالجائز دون المستحيل ، فانه مطالب بفعل أو اضراب عن فعل ، وكلاهما جائزان ، وكما لا يجرى على العبد من تقدير بارئه ، الا ما يجوز . فكذلك لا يطالبه الا بما يجوز
The third pillar: that the commanded thing must be possible in itself, in existence and occurrence. So it is not permissible for the obligation (taklif) to come regarding combining two opposites, or being in two places at one time. And it is impossible for the command to come regarding disbelief in God, Blessed and Exalted, because from the necessity of conceptualizing the command is that the commanded one understands the command and knows the Commander. How can it be conceived that with knowledge of God, the Commander, there be ignorance of Him? That is a kind of combining two opposites. So these three pillars have emerged upon a single principle which is the foundation of the creed (qa‘idat al-‘aqidah): that the servant is required only with what is possible, not the impossible. For he is required with an action or refraining from an action, and both are possible. And just as nothing occurs to the servant from the decree of his Creator except what is possible, so too He does not require him except with what is possible.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage defines the third pillar: the commanded act must be intrinsically possible (mumkin) in both essence and occurrence. Juwayni gives examples: God does not command combining two opposites (like being in two places at once) because that is impossible. Nor does He command disbelief in Himself, because that would require ignorance of the Commander, which is contradictory—knowing God yet disbelieving in Him is like combining opposites. Thus, all three pillars (intellect, possible act, and reward/punishment? Actually here it says these three pillars—he refers to the first three discussed in the book, including the first not in this passage) rest on a single principle: the servant is only required with what is possible (ja’iz), not impossible. The servant's obligation is either to do or to refrain, both of which are possible. Just as God's decree only brings about what is possible, so His commands only obligate what is possible.

Key Terms
ممكن
مستحيل
جمع الضدين
جائز
قاعدة العقيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God can command something impossible to test faith.
Correction: Juwayni argues that commanding the impossible is contradictory to the nature of obligation; the servant must be capable of understanding and performing the command. Commanding disbelief, for example, would require ignorance of the Commander, which is impossible given knowledge of Him.

Checkpoint

A) It is possible if the person is ignorant
B) It is impossible because it combines knowledge of God with ignorance
C) It is a test of faith
D) It is permissible if the person is a sinner

Key Takeaway
The third pillar ensures that Allah's commands are always within the realm of possibility for the human being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel overwhelmed by a religious obligation (like prayer or fasting), do you truly believe that Allah has not commanded you beyond your ability? What would change if you internalized this principle?
?
This passage teaches that commanding disbelief is impossible because it would require contradictory knowledge. How does understanding God's perfect wisdom and justice in commanding only what is possible affect your relationship with Him – especially when you struggle with your faith?
?
Juwayni concludes that the servant is only required with what is possible. Does your life reflect a trust in Allah's commands, or do you sometimes doubt that He knows your limits? What step can you take today to align your heart with this belief?

The Deviant Views on Reward and Punishment
Juwayni describes deviant views on reward and punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the deviant views that assert obligatory reward and punishment upon God, and the disagreements among them.

Core Idea
Some sects claim God must reward obedience and punish disobedience, but they disagree on details.

Juwayni begins the fourth pillar by describing the flawed positions of deviant sects. He notes their disagreements: some say punishment is obligatory and pardon only after repentance; others allow pardon but still insist reward is obligatory. These views set the stage for the correct Ash'ari position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the strong language Juwayni uses ('prolonged nonsense').
2
Identify the two deviant groups
The passage mentions two groups. One group holds that punishment is obligatory and pardon only after repentance. The other group holds that pardon is permissible but reward is still obligatory. Find these positions.
Both groups agree that reward is obligatory, but differ on punishment.
3
Understand Juwayni's dismissal
Juwayni dismisses these views as 'prolonged nonsense' and 'exalted is He above their saying'. Reflect on why he uses such strong language.
He considers attributing obligation to God as a fundamental error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الركن الرابع : يتعلق بالثواب والعقاب . ذهبت طوائف من أهل الزيغ والضلال ، الى أن العبد اذا أطاع ربه ، وجب على الله تبارك وتعالى أن يثيبه وجوب الحكمة . فإن عصاه اضطربوا في حكم الاله . فقال قائلون : يجب على الله تبارك وتعالى ، أن يعاقبه ، ولا يجوز أن يعفو عنه ما لم يتب فإن تاب وجب عليه قبول توبته .. وذهب آخرون : الى أن العفو مسوغ في العقل .. والثواب واجب على الله - تبارك وتعالى ، عن قولهم علوا كبيرا - من هذيان طويل ، وصار أهل الحق قاطبة : الى أنه لا يجب على الله شيء . فان أتاب وأنعم فيفضله ، وإن عاقب فبعدله . والدليل القاطع في تحقيق ما ارتضاه أهل الحق ، أن الوجوب انما يتحقق في حق من لو فرض منه ترك الواجب لاستحق الذم واللائمة ، ولو ليم أو عوقب ، لناله ضرر ، والرب تبارك وتعالى يتقدس عن قبول الضر والنفع ، ولا يتحقق تفاوت الأفعال في حكمه كما سبق . ومما يقطع مادة كلامهم : أن العبادات التي يقيمها العبد لا تفي بالنعم التي تتوفر عليه من ربه ، ناجزا ، وهى تقع شكرا لأنعم الله تبارك وتعالى بل لا تفى بأقلها فاذا وقعت شكرا عوضا عما تعجل من نعم الله ، فكيف يستمر في حكم العقل استحقاق الثواب على أعمال وقعت عوضا عن نعيم توفاه العبد ؟ ثم قالوا : ليس على أهل الجنان شكر النعيمها . فانه عوض أعمال العبد ، وليس للمعوض عوض . فمن أضل سبيلا ممن يوجب على الله تبارك وتعالى ثواب أعمال العبد ، وهى عوض ما ينجز من النعم ، ولم يوجب على العبد ، شكر الثواب غدا لكونه عوضا ؟ ثم من زعم أن العقل يدل على استحقاق العقل بكفر
The fourth pillar: it pertains to reward and punishment. Sects among the people of deviation and error held that when the servant obeys his Lord, it is obligatory upon God, Blessed and Exalted, to reward him, by necessity of wisdom. And if he disobeys Him, they disagreed about the divine judgment. Some said: It is obligatory upon God, Blessed and Exalted, to punish him, and it is not permissible to pardon him unless he repents; if he repents, acceptance of his repentance is obligatory. Others held that pardon is permissible in reason, and reward is obligatory upon God—Exalted is He above their saying, a great exaltation—from prolonged nonsense. The people of truth (ahl al-haqq) unanimously held that nothing is obligatory upon God. So if He guideth and bestoweth favors, it is by His grace; and if He punisheth, it is by His justice. The decisive proof in confirming what the people of truth have chosen: indeed, obligation is only realized concerning one of whom if he were to leave the obligatory, he would deserve blame and reproach; and if he were blamed or punished, harm would befall him. But the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, is transcendent above receiving harm or benefit, and there is no realization of variation of actions in His ruling, as previously mentioned. And among what cuts the root of their speech: indeed, the acts of worship that the servant performs do not fulfill the blessings that flow to him from his Lord, immediately; they occur as gratitude for the blessings of God, Blessed and Exalted—rather, they do not fulfill even the least of them. So when they occur as gratitude in exchange for what was hastened of God's blessings, how can it continue in the judgment of reason to deserve reward for actions that occurred as exchange for bliss that the servant has already received? Then they said: The people of paradise are not obligated to be grateful for its bliss, for it is an exchange for the servant's deeds, and there is no exchange for exchange. So who is more astray than one who makes obligatory upon God, Blessed and Exalted, reward for the servant's deeds, which are exchange for the blessings conferred, yet does not make obligatory upon the servant gratitude for the reward tomorrow because it is an exchange? Then he who claims that reason indicates deserving... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of deviant views on reward and punishment, and a clarification of the position of Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth). Juwayni describes sects that say God must reward the obedient and punish the disobedient (with some allowing pardon after repentance). Others say pardon is permissible but reward is still obligatory. The People of Truth, al-Ash'ari, hold that nothing is obligatory upon God. Reward is by His grace, punishment by His justice. Juwayni provides two proofs. First: obligation implies that the one who fails to do the obligatory deserves blame and would be harmed by punishment, but God is transcendent above receiving harm or benefit. Second: acts of worship do not repay God's blessings—they are mere gratitude for blessings already received. Thus they cannot be a basis for demanding further reward; they are exchange (compensation) for those blessings. Paradise itself is an exchange for deeds, so there is no compensation for compensation. Thus those who demand reward from God are inconsistent: they demand reward for deeds that are already compensation, yet do not demand gratitude for the reward itself. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing.

Key Terms
ثواب
عقاب
وجوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to reward good deeds and punish bad deeds.
Correction: Juwayni and Ahl al-Haqq hold that nothing is obligatory upon God; reward is by grace, punishment by justice.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do both deviant groups agree upon?
A) Punishment is obligatory for disobedience
B) Reward is obligatory upon God for obedience
C) Pardon is always permissible
D) Repentance is never accepted

Key Takeaway
Deviant views impose obligation on God, but they disagree on the specifics of punishment and pardon.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Juwayni calls these views 'nonsense.' If you were to apply that label to your own assumptions about what God 'must' do, what would you have to change? Are you ready to submit to God's absolute freedom, even if it means letting go of your own sense of justice?

The Ash'ari Position: Nothing Is Obligatory Upon God
Juwayni states the Ash'ari position and provides the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that nothing is obligatory upon God, and the first proof from the impossibility of blame and harm applying to Him.

Core Idea
God is not bound by obligation; His acts are by grace or justice.

After dismissing the deviant views, Juwayni presents the unanimous position of Ahl al-Haqq: nothing is obligatory upon God. He then provides a decisive proof: obligation implies liability to blame and harm, which cannot apply to the transcendent God.

Guided Path
1
Read the position statement
Notice the concise statement: 'nothing is obligatory upon God'. Then note the consequence: reward is by grace, punishment by justice.
This is the core Ash'ari creed on the matter.
2
Analyze the proof
The proof begins by defining obligation: it only applies to one who would deserve blame for leaving it, and who would suffer harm if punished. Reflect on why this cannot apply to God.
Why does Juwayni focus on harm? Because God is transcendent, immune to harm or benefit.
3
Relate to previous topics
Juwayni references what was said before about the lack of variation in God's ruling. Connect this to the idea that actions are not inherently good or evil.
This reinforces that nothing obligates God from outside.

الركن الرابع : يتعلق بالثواب والعقاب . ذهبت طوائف من أهل الزيغ والضلال ، الى أن العبد اذا أطاع ربه ، وجب على الله تبارك وتعالى أن يثيبه وجوب الحكمة . فإن عصاه اضطربوا في حكم الاله . فقال قائلون : يجب على الله تبارك وتعالى ، أن يعاقبه ، ولا يجوز أن يعفو عنه ما لم يتب فإن تاب وجب عليه قبول توبته .. وذهب آخرون : الى أن العفو مسوغ في العقل .. والثواب واجب على الله - تبارك وتعالى ، عن قولهم علوا كبيرا - من هذيان طويل ، وصار أهل الحق قاطبة : الى أنه لا يجب على الله شيء . فان أتاب وأنعم فيفضله ، وإن عاقب فبعدله . والدليل القاطع في تحقيق ما ارتضاه أهل الحق ، أن الوجوب انما يتحقق في حق من لو فرض منه ترك الواجب لاستحق الذم واللائمة ، ولو ليم أو عوقب ، لناله ضرر ، والرب تبارك وتعالى يتقدس عن قبول الضر والنفع ، ولا يتحقق تفاوت الأفعال في حكمه كما سبق . ومما يقطع مادة كلامهم : أن العبادات التي يقيمها العبد لا تفي بالنعم التي تتوفر عليه من ربه ، ناجزا ، وهى تقع شكرا لأنعم الله تبارك وتعالى بل لا تفى بأقلها فاذا وقعت شكرا عوضا عما تعجل من نعم الله ، فكيف يستمر في حكم العقل استحقاق الثواب على أعمال وقعت عوضا عن نعيم توفاه العبد ؟ ثم قالوا : ليس على أهل الجنان شكر النعيمها . فانه عوض أعمال العبد ، وليس للمعوض عوض . فمن أضل سبيلا ممن يوجب على الله تبارك وتعالى ثواب أعمال العبد ، وهى عوض ما ينجز من النعم ، ولم يوجب على العبد ، شكر الثواب غدا لكونه عوضا ؟ ثم من زعم أن العقل يدل على استحقاق العقل بكفر
The fourth pillar: it pertains to reward and punishment. Sects among the people of deviation and error held that when the servant obeys his Lord, it is obligatory upon God, Blessed and Exalted, to reward him, by necessity of wisdom. And if he disobeys Him, they disagreed about the divine judgment. Some said: It is obligatory upon God, Blessed and Exalted, to punish him, and it is not permissible to pardon him unless he repents; if he repents, acceptance of his repentance is obligatory. Others held that pardon is permissible in reason, and reward is obligatory upon God—Exalted is He above their saying, a great exaltation—from prolonged nonsense. The people of truth (ahl al-haqq) unanimously held that nothing is obligatory upon God. So if He guideth and bestoweth favors, it is by His grace; and if He punisheth, it is by His justice. The decisive proof in confirming what the people of truth have chosen: indeed, obligation is only realized concerning one of whom if he were to leave the obligatory, he would deserve blame and reproach; and if he were blamed or punished, harm would befall him. But the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, is transcendent above receiving harm or benefit, and there is no realization of variation of actions in His ruling, as previously mentioned. And among what cuts the root of their speech: indeed, the acts of worship that the servant performs do not fulfill the blessings that flow to him from his Lord, immediately; they occur as gratitude for the blessings of God, Blessed and Exalted—rather, they do not fulfill even the least of them. So when they occur as gratitude in exchange for what was hastened of God's blessings, how can it continue in the judgment of reason to deserve reward for actions that occurred as exchange for bliss that the servant has already received? Then they said: The people of paradise are not obligated to be grateful for its bliss, for it is an exchange for the servant's deeds, and there is no exchange for exchange. So who is more astray than one who makes obligatory upon God, Blessed and Exalted, reward for the servant's deeds, which are exchange for the blessings conferred, yet does not make obligatory upon the servant gratitude for the reward tomorrow because it is an exchange? Then he who claims that reason indicates deserving... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of deviant views on reward and punishment, and a clarification of the position of Ahl al-Haqq (People of Truth). Juwayni describes sects that say God must reward the obedient and punish the disobedient (with some allowing pardon after repentance). Others say pardon is permissible but reward is still obligatory. The People of Truth, al-Ash'ari, hold that nothing is obligatory upon God. Reward is by His grace, punishment by His justice. Juwayni provides two proofs. First: obligation implies that the one who fails to do the obligatory deserves blame and would be harmed by punishment, but God is transcendent above receiving harm or benefit. Second: acts of worship do not repay God's blessings—they are mere gratitude for blessings already received. Thus they cannot be a basis for demanding further reward; they are exchange (compensation) for those blessings. Paradise itself is an exchange for deeds, so there is no compensation for compensation. Thus those who demand reward from God are inconsistent: they demand reward for deeds that are already compensation, yet do not demand gratitude for the reward itself. The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing.

فضل
عدل
أهل الحق

A) Because God is merciful
B) Because obligation requires the possibility of blame and harm, which cannot affect God
C) Because human deeds are insufficient
D) Because God has no will

Key Takeaway
Obligation cannot apply to God because it implies vulnerability to blame and harm, contradicting divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Second Proof: Acts of Worship as Gratitude
Juwayni presents the second proof based on exchange and gratitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that acts of worship are insufficient to repay God's blessings, so demanding reward is inconsistent.

Core Idea
Our acts of worship are mere gratitude for blessings already received, not a basis for demanding reward.

Juwayni provides a second argument: our acts of worship cannot repay God's endless blessings; they are only gratitude in exchange for those blessings. Therefore, demanding reward for them is like demanding compensation for compensation, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the key premises: acts of worship do not fulfill blessings; they are gratitude (shukr). They are an exchange (iwad) for blessings.
This is a more detailed refutation.
2
Understand the concept of exchange
Juwayni argues that worship is compensation (iwad) for blessings already received. Therefore, it cannot be a basis for demanding further reward; that would be like asking for compensation for compensation.
He uses the example of paradise being an exchange for deeds.
3
Reflect on the inconsistency
Juwayni points out that those who demand reward from God do not demand gratitude from people for the reward itself. This shows their argument is self-contradictory.
The text breaks off, but the main point is clear.
